    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer13933217352475834556">Una coleccion que adapta grandes obras y autores de la literatura escrita en espanol.Cada titulo incluye:Una breve presentacion de la vida y obra del autor. El texto adaptado. Una seleccion de partes significativas de la obra en su version original. Una lista de palabras, agrupando en la misma entrada a las de la misma familia lexica. Una guia de comprension lectora que ay</span>
  <span id="freeText13933217352475834556" style="display:none">Una coleccion que adapta grandes obras y autores de la literatura escrita en espanol.Cada titulo incluye:Una breve presentacion de la vida y obra del autor. El texto adaptado. Una seleccion de partes significativas de la obra en su version original. Una lista de palabras, agrupando en la misma entrada a las de la misma familia lexica. Una guia de comprension lectora que ayuda a elaborar una ficha resumen de la lectura del libro. Para que el lector pueda descubrir el placer del texto original hay una seleccion de partes significativas de la obra en V.O. (version original). Una vez leida toda la adaptacion, se pueden seguir con la V.O. los momentos principales del relato.</span>

<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/668984-the-don-quixote-has-a-huge-secret-to-tell">The Don Quixote has a huge secret to tell. Do you know what this secret is? I know it after 5 years of investigation. I know what Francis Bacon with his crew of 5 writers has to tell. Cervantes is just a figurehead, he did not write this intelligent work, he is just a testaferro. The whole story is an esoteric initiation, besides he tells where the Holy Greal is hidden. Can you believe it? 
jettie h. van den Boom</a>
</div>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/98748022?book_show_action=true">Apr 16, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kalliope" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kalliope" href="/user/show/3593962-kalliope">Kalliope</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9788437622149" href="/book/show/69618.Don_Quijote_de_la_Mancha_I">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3593962-kalliope?shelf=spain">spain</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3593962-kalliope?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3593962-kalliope?shelf=fiction-spanish">fiction-spanish</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3593962-kalliope?shelf=cervantes">cervantes</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer98748022" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2125217084730724539"><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1469297630i/19803188._SX540_.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/><br/>You, my GR-Friends and Readers must be wondering what is this cheese doing at the head of the review. And I, the fictitious Kalliope, invite you to cut a slice and help yourself and savour the flavours of Manchego cheese. Buts since you can’t grab and eat the one displayed on your screen, then I suggest you take a substitute, some Edam, or Cheddar, or any other you have in your fridge, and then think and imagine that you are taking cheese from La Mancha, from the land of Don Quijote (DQ). For ac</span>
  <span id="freeText2125217084730724539" style="display:none"><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1469297630i/19803188._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><br>You, my GR-Friends and Readers must be wondering what is this cheese doing at the head of the review. And I, the fictitious Kalliope, invite you to cut a slice and help yourself and savour the flavours of Manchego cheese. Buts since you can’t grab and eat the one displayed on your screen, then I suggest you take a substitute, some Edam, or Cheddar, or any other you have in your fridge, and then think and imagine that you are taking cheese from La Mancha, from the land of Don Quijote (DQ). For according to him what you imagine is what exists.<br><br><blockquote> I imagine that everything I say is true, no more and no less, and I depict her (Dulcinea) in my imagination as I wish her to be in beauty and distinction. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[<i>Yo imagino que todo lo que digo es así, sin que sobre ni falte nada, y píntola en mi imaginación como la deseo</i>&gt;/spoiler&gt; <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>
</blockquote> <br><br>This would work with the cheeses also.<br><br>My father--and this is for real--, who had read DQ four times by the time he was eleven-, would tell me that when reading Cervantes’ masterwork one had to keep on the side a platter of Manchego cheese and a carafe of red wine- Tinto, and nibble and sip whenever the characters ate cheese and drank wine. I used to think this was a joke but in this last, more careful and pondering, read of DQ’s First part (1605) <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[I am reviewing here only the First Part – often published together, the second was written much later and published in 1616 <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>, I realized that what seemed jest on his part was a literary comment with fundamental implications. <br><br>For as this novel is about the complex and fluid relationship between reality and fiction, and the dangers of fiction if you let it dominate your imagination, the best antidote is cheese. Unless you engage your body and attend to its physiological needs regularly while you read, you run the danger of dissociating from your body and of losing your wits.<br><br>So, please, help yourself now to a slice and take a sip. I keep on my side a dish because behind that spirally kalliope there is a person who feels hungry and thirsty some times. And better stick to the wine and do not dream of trying the <b>Fierrabrás Balm</b>, or you will regret it. As did poor Sancho.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1469298184i/19803304._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Food has a literary role and helps in distinguishing the various literary genres from which this novel is made. For example, <b>Pedro</b>, the common shepherd, eats, while <b>Grisóstomo</b>, the literary student who placidly and harmoniously takes care of sheep, does not. And had Quijote been called <b>Don Gigote</b> (meaning 'meat stew'), as Dorotea once thought, maybe things would have gone easier for him. Cheese can be a good substitute for jewellery – as Sancho reports when he tells DQ of his phony meeting with Dulcinea. The practice of chivalric novels was to offer a precious stone to the messenger as a token of appreciation, but Sancho is proud that instead he got a better deal: cheese of pure sheep milk (<i>queso ovejuno</i>).<br><br>DQ as a model knight-errant, does not need to feed. When Sancho offers him to share his victuals, DQ elegantly refuses – he has never read in any of his chivalric novels that the knights, with the exception of attending enrapturing banquets, do bother with eating. They just are in communion with nature and the wild weeds and herbs are sufficient to nourish their souls and courage. Sancho then offers him some nuts (nuts for the nut?), but DQ, meekly insinuates that bread and sardines would be as good as the chivalric food and finally accepts Sancho’s offer without further ado. And at another point DQ acknowledges, after having lost some teeth in a fight that will make his eating much more difficult, that teeth are better than diamonds. Life in an imagined world is not always easy.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1469298342i/19803320._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>For DQ’s goals require demanding dedication. When he sees the flock of sheep that make his fantasy fly and recites one of the most memorable passages in the novel, evoking all the grand deeds of his army of heroes, there comes a point when his grandiose personalities seem to dissolve and take the shape of sheep. He needs reinforcement against the enchantments brought by the conjurers and wizards and drinks some of the Fierrabrás Balm. The reader wishes that Sancho had given him, instead, some Tinto to drink.<br><br>And it is food, and a bit of rest, what seems to calm down DQ when he enters in a more dangerous kind of madness--a sort of dissociative disorder that only happens at the beginning of the book--when DQ imagines himself to be someone else. Once he thinks he is <b>Reinaldos de Montalbán</b>, from Boiardo’s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/756484.Orlando_Innamorato__Orlando_in_Love_" title="Orlando Innamorato (Orlando in Love) by Matteo Maria Boiardo" rel="nofollow noopener">Orlando Innamorato</a>, and another time he thinks he is <b>Valdovinos</b> (from the Carolingian Romancier of the Marchess of Mantua) and yet another time, he is convinced he is <b>Abindarráez</b>. That he could not be Abindarráez I knew well, because a few months ago I attended the performance of the <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3177178.Historia_del_Abencerraje_y_la_hermosa_Jarifa" title="Historia del Abencerraje y la hermosa Jarifa by Anonymous" rel="nofollow noopener">Historia del Abencerraje y la hermosa Jarifa</a>, and I had gone for tapas beforehand and eaten some cheese, so I was immune to the fantasies to which DQ fell. As for Valdovinos, again, had Sancho been able to show him the Valdovinos wine…. But maybe he did, for those dissociative fits do not repeat themselves.<br><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1469297724i/19803221._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><br>At stake then, is DQ’s puzzle created by his broad reading <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Martin de Riquer, the editor of my wonderful edition, expands on this <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. He could not distinguish between three kinds of writing: histories or chronicles that recorded facts; ‘historical fiction’ of various forms but still based on real people; and the then pervasive ‘chivalric novel’ made out of extravagant fantasies. The origins of this confusion are partly semantic. The Spanish word <i>Novela</i> (novel – originating in the Italian <i>novella</i>) was relatively modern. For prose works longer than a novella, Spanish could not use the French <i>Roman</i> or Italian <i>Romanzo</i> because <i>Romance</i> was a particular kind of poem. Chronicles, fictional stories and the chivalric works all used the word Historia (both 'history' and 'novel'). <br><br>DQ, as well as the Innkeeper towards the end of the novel, have been all confused with the various heroes, whether these were the <b>Emperor Charles V</b>, or <b>Rodrigo Díaz de Vivar</b>, or the real <b>Rolando</b> defeated in Roncesvalles, or the transformed and fantastical <b>Orlando</b> from both Boiardo’s and Ariosto’s works. DQ is more impressed by the <i>Knight of the Ardent Sword</i> (chivalry work by Feliciano de Silva) than by Rodrigo Díaz de Vivar, El Cid (apart from the Epic poem there were several chronicles). Similarly, the Innkeeper also thinks that <i>Felixmarte de Hircania</i>, who with one stroke could cut in half five giants, is much more impressive than El Gran Capitán (<b>Gonzalo Fernández de Córdoba</b> – the Great Captain – who defeated the French in the last Italian wars).<br><br>May be it would have helped, at least with El Gran Capitán, if Sancho had been able to show both the Innkeeper and DQ (who had also expressed not to be too impressed by him) that this heroic captain was so real that he has his own cheese.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1469297929i/19803252._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>If we can conceive of this novel as a game on the borderlines between fiction and reality, representation and truth, game that Cervantes would explore even further when he took up the second part of his work, it comes as a tease to the reader how he warns the ‘author’ of the book, the fictional <b>Cide Hamete Berengeli</b>, not to stray from what should be his goal, and not hold back parts of the truth. The constant and deliberate presence of food in the novel, and the care to feed his characters regularly--in spite of DQ' occasional refusals--, is part of Cervantes' aim not to stray away from reality and make his fiction and his characters credible.<br><br><br>The best for pursuing this aim, then, is just to cut another piece of Manchego and finish the glass of Tinto.<br><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1469298105i/19803279._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></span>
  <a data-text-id="2125217084730724539" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_51931271" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/51931271" />
    <a title="David" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/166376-david"><img alt="David" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1345482402p2/166376.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/51931271?book_show_action=true">Apr 08, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David" href="/user/show/166376-david">David</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781406791723" href="/book/show/1382972.Don_Quixote_de_La_Mancha_Vol_1">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/166376-david?shelf=read-in-2009">read-in-2009</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer51931271" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7760989931658296605"><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1379730349i/17067.jpg' width='180' height='280' alt='Quijote lookalike' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>First, an organizational note. I actually read this in parallel, in the original Spanish, and in the Penguin Classics English translation by J.M. Cohen. Anyone who is interested can follow my tortured progress through Book I at the link below:<br/><br/><a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/49081259'>http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/...</a><br/><br/>In this review, I will attempt a coherent summary of my reaction to Book I, and in the process try to justify my two-star rating.<br/><br/>Let me start by saying that I really gave it my best shot. I can't think of another book</span>
  <span id="freeText7760989931658296605" style="display:none"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1379730349i/17067.jpg" width="180" height="280" alt="Quijote lookalike" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>First, an organizational note. I actually read this in parallel, in the original Spanish, and in the Penguin Classics English translation by J.M. Cohen. Anyone who is interested can follow my tortured progress through Book I at the link below:<br><br><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/49081259">http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/...</a><br><br>In this review, I will attempt a coherent summary of my reaction to Book I, and in the process try to justify my two-star rating.<br><br>Let me start by saying that I really gave it my best shot. I can't think of another book that I've read as closely. Read it in Spanish AND in English. Even - to keep myself honest - tried summarizing as I went, in deathless doggerel (<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/story/show/15609.The_Doggerel_File?chapter=10" rel="nofollow noopener"> QUIK QUIXOTE </a>). I've spent a month of my life with this book - it's been a mild obsession. <br><br>Why? Oh, I don't know. Can we ever satisfactorily explain why we choose to give ourselves over to any specific whim? I'm here, in Madrid. I'm studying Spanish at a school that's called "Don Quijote". The time just seemed right. And I had a strong feeling that it was going to be now or never. <br><br>But a coherent summary of my reaction eludes me, frankly. This book alternately amused me and bored me to tears. There are a couple of places where I laughed out loud. But mostly I just wanted it to be over. I wasn't about to quit. But it felt awfully like a penance, much of the time. <br><br>Some random observations, for which I am forced to resort to the dreaded list of bullet items:<br><br>1. The Spanish was often more fun to read than the English. Cervantes and Shakespeare were contemporaries (down to the urban legend (?) of having died on the same day). But Spanish has changed considerably less in the intervening 400 years than English. There were enough archaic words that I did feel reading the translation was a necessary check, but it was surprisingly straightforward in Spanish, and - traduttori, tradittori - one felt closer to the original story. So I definitely enjoyed that aspect of reading the book - it felt like a real confirmation that all the Spanish classes have paid off.<br><br>2. I have a good general understanding of the book's place in literary history, and so was willing to cut it some slack - that is to say, not to judge it as one would a modern novel. That said, I still can't avoid saying that I found it enormously clunky. The first couple of hundred pages were annoyingly episodic and formulaic - addled Don meets (a)windmills (b)yokels (c)sheep (d)funeral mourners - take your pick - is confused, through a <i> hilarious misunderstanding </i> (but see point 5 below) attacks them, gets the worst of the dustup, and ends in the ditch. <br><br>3. Things improved a little in the second half (of Book I), when some of the protagonists other than Don and Sancho start to appear on a recurring basis. But don't look for in-depth characterization, or much character development to speak of. Cervantes is no Shakespeare. <br>OK. Let me repeat that for the benefit of each and every one of my Spanish teachers, though I love them dearly. People - you are completely fucking delusional! CERVANTES IS NO SHAKESPEARE. When you make this comparison, you just make me want to resort to actual physical violence. I've read Shakespeare and, dudes, CERVANTES IS NO FUCKING SHAKESPEARE. There's more subtlety, insight, and depth of understanding of human nature in almost any single Shakespeare play (OK, "Titus Andronicus" is a little weird, but there are still over thirty to choose from) than in this entire first volume. Not to mention a superabundance of the most gorgeous language, though - to be fair - I can't quite fairly judge Cervantes on this score.<br><br>4. When the plot isn't being all episodic, it's not really any great shakes either. Mick is altogether too heavy-handed with the AMAZING COINCIDENCE method of plot resolution. Man, you wouldn't believe who all happens to mosey on by the same remote Manchegan inn, just in time to tie up a dangling plot thread. I dunno. It all seems more than a little -- lazy. <br>Though I guess (and I feel like I'm really bending over backwards to give Mick the benefit of the doubt here - why is that - in retroactive justification of the time I invested reading this damned book?) maybe I'm applying modern criteria and expectations here. It's not as if all of Shakespeare's plots were entirely plausible either.<br><br>5. Humor. Ah, yes. One of literature's great comic masterpieces. Well, excuse me, if I fail to climb on this particular bandwagon. I'm sorry, but I just don't get it. Let's be quite clear - the humor, such as it is, is almost exclusively slapstick of the broadest kind. If you like watching circus clowns do pratfalls, or if your dream television weekend is a Three Stooges marathon, then maybe you´ll laugh like a drain. But if you don't really find slapstick all that hilarious, or take vicarious pleasure in taunting and jeering at a deranged person, you will, as I was, wonder what all the fuss is about. (Yes: I acknowledge that there is some wit in the book's initial premise - a person addled by too much book-reading. But lemme tellya, it gets old awful quick. It really does).<br>One goodreads reviewer tells us, with no apparent irony, that this is the funniest book he has ever read in his life. A statement that can only be literally true if he is a shut-in with no access to a library.<br><br>6. And on the subject of those ratings by other goodreads reviewers. De gustibus non disputandum est (i.e. diff'rent strokes..) etc. But really, folks, I'm having a hard time swallowing it. An average rating of 4.69? 102 5-star ratings? <br>Might it not be possible, just faintly possible, that we have a slight case of what one might call "classic intimidation" going on? The (perhaps unconscious) fear that people may think less of one for not appreciating one of the world's designated literary classics? Did all these apparently rabid Quixote enthusiasts - and how can I put this delicately - ACTUALLY READ THE WHOLE BOOK? All of it, without cheating? All those pastoral poems by the love-besotted shepherds? The entire soporific "Tale of Foolish Curiosity". The limited empirical data available suggests that maybe close attention was not paid - a mere 9.9% of respondents chose the correct answer to the goodreads quiz question about the "fulling hammers".<br>Just sayin'. I has my doubts.<br><br>7. Because, here's the thing. Large swaths of this book are intrinsically unreadable. No, I mean it. You read a page. Your eyes glaze over. You try it again. Same phenomenon. Cycle and repeat. <br>I humbly submit that the stuff in which Cervantes is engaged in direct spoofing of the knight-errant genre - all the stuff about Amadis of Gaul, the Don's argument with the Canon, the priest's adjudication of the various volumes in the Don's library, not to mention the interminable pastoral interludes with lovelorn shepherds and damsels dressed as shepherdesses could be considered <b>interesting</b> only by the most desperate of graduate students in need of a dissertation topic. For anyone not engaged in abstruse academic investigation it's a freaking snoozefest. <br><br>Did I enjoy "Don Quixote, Book I"? Only very sporadically. Do I consider it one of the world's great books? Absolutely not. Will I read Book II? Oddly enough, probably yes. <br><br>But not this trip. And probably not this year. Let the Don lie slumbering back home in La Mancha. Myself, I hope to travel to Chile in July, and Argentina in August. I think that other, more appealing, opportunities will arise to extend my knowledge of literature in Spanish.<br><br>So there you have it. Sorry. I told you that I probably wouldn't manage an entirely coherent review.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7760989931658296605" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2989482540" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2989482540" />
    <a title="Stephen P" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11602286-stephen-p"><img alt="Stephen P" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1461732018p2/11602286.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2989482540" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2134284263590768094">The stars move toward an infinity not to be counted. Yet so accessible. They shift over making a space for me around the campfire as we listen to Cervantes’ narrator tell us tales nested within tales. The emergence of modern tropes. I follow the Don who will not cease to follow his life source; his imagination; his seeing the actual reality agreed upon lacking. Robed in humor there is a biting edge of honing books as the source of life. The inner life over the outer life? Breeding laughter while</span>
  <span id="freeText2134284263590768094" style="display:none">The stars move toward an infinity not to be counted. Yet so accessible. They shift over making a space for me around the campfire as we listen to Cervantes’ narrator tell us tales nested within tales. The emergence of modern tropes. I follow the Don who will not cease to follow his life source; his imagination; his seeing the actual reality agreed upon lacking. Robed in humor there is a biting edge of honing books as the source of life. The inner life over the outer life? Breeding laughter while breeding thought.<br /><br />What a reading experience! I am so glad that I now have this volume as a part of me. What I am perplexed about is that Volume 2 was written without permission by someone else and therefore a decade later Cervantes writes his; a reaction or a creative urge. Does anyone know the answer. It would help me greatly in deciding whether to go onto Volume 2. </span>

<div id="review_2890686688" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2890686688" />
    <a title="Chris M.H" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/86729658-chris-m-h"><img alt="Chris M.H" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1572799926p2/86729658.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2890686688?book_show_action=true">Jul 10, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chris M.H" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chris M.H" href="/user/show/86729658-chris-m-h">Chris M.H</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781406791723" href="/book/show/1382972.Don_Quixote_de_La_Mancha_Vol_1">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/86729658-chris-m-h?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2890686688" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11801593871479621781">Such a beautiful and hilarious novel.<br/><br/>It has heap loads of danger, insults, foolishness, comedy & love. It’s all stories interwoven within stories and it’s just brilliant!<br/><br/>I think the part I love most about this book is the affection each character has to someone else. No one person seems without direction to send their passion and receive in turn. The long scene in the Inn on the way back from Don Quixote’s isolation in the wilderness and following deception in returning home by Dorothea and comp</span>
  <span id="freeText11801593871479621781" style="display:none">Such a beautiful and hilarious novel.<br /><br />It has heap loads of danger, insults, foolishness, comedy &amp; love. It’s all stories interwoven within stories and it’s just brilliant!<br /><br />I think the part I love most about this book is the affection each character has to someone else. No one person seems without direction to send their passion and receive in turn. The long scene in the Inn on the way back from Don Quixote’s isolation in the wilderness and following deception in returning home by Dorothea and company is a wonderful example of this. I found the episode in its entirety so engaging and exciting as you get to hear of masked arrivals, Don’s imaginations of giant slaying, brothers being sent out by a prodigal but loving father - only for one of them to be caught and used as a slave who then gets aided in escape by a beautiful Moor who eventually ends up at the Inn with the rest. All of this passage is so beautifully interwoven and expressed, with the odd intermittent humour of Quixote that it probably surpasses anything I’ve ever read.<br /><br />I’ve seen somewhere that some readers of this book have found it ‘lacking in vivid power’ and it’s quite true. Very few parts of the first half of the book I thought expressed themselves with much brilliance, fuel for the imagination so to speak. Whether it’s to do with the writing style or the pace with which the characters move from one adventure to the next I can’t tell, perhaps a mixture of the two and some but it’s certainly lacking. The odd thing is though I didn’t feel it detracted from the elegance and power of the novel. Far from it, you soon begin to learn that anything could be cropping up around the next corner and it’s likely to be far detached from what was expected.<br /><br />Very long and complex book but I'm very much looking forward to reading the 2nd part in the near future. Brilliant.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11801593871479621781" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/698323232?book_show_action=true">Aug 18, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Marie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Marie" href="/user/show/5157049-marie">Marie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9788437622149" href="/book/show/69618.Don_Quijote_de_la_Mancha_I">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5157049-marie?shelf=favorite-books">favorite-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer698323232" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9147711290279776284">It's hard to classify and to explain what this book is about. <br/><br/>In all honesty, one could describe the plot basic points, and still not get a grasp of how complete this book is in every sense. It has helped me to discover a love for this kind of reality, standing in the thin line between dream in reality, and the problems of knowledge among many other things. <br/><br/>One of the first books of this style I have had the pleasure of reading was François Rabelais, "Pantagruel" which for sure have some points </span>
  <span id="freeText9147711290279776284" style="display:none">It's hard to classify and to explain what this book is about. <br /><br />In all honesty, one could describe the plot basic points, and still not get a grasp of how complete this book is in every sense. It has helped me to discover a love for this kind of reality, standing in the thin line between dream in reality, and the problems of knowledge among many other things. <br /><br />One of the first books of this style I have had the pleasure of reading was François Rabelais, "Pantagruel" which for sure have some points in common with this one, though Cervantes' first part of Don Quixote relies more in violence rather than sexual content, there's a lot of grotesque elements, even in language and references to popular culture. <br /><br />The doubt between what's real and what's fiction is not only shown in Don Quixote's case, but in many characters belonging to lower sectors of society, as in the continuous debate concerning the meaning of literature, considering that its content is often taken as true by just being approved by royal authorities.<br /><br />The constant elements of reality and contemporary events, as well as references to the life of the authors and the depiction of the moslim world give it a richness hardly seen in other books. <br /><br />There's a lot of importance in love's role in all its forms, from marriage to loyalty as expected in a friendship. <br /> <br />The female characters are strong and decided, willing to accomplish their goals, from making a man marry them as they accorded, to successfully convert to Catholicism for love to the Virgin Mary. <br /><br />One of the most interesting features is that, we manage to infer that while Don Quixote's actions have his madness as their origin, they aren't a product of bad intentions. <br /><br />The often recurring conflicts in which Don Quixote ends up beaten by the world who refuses to take part in his old mannered, highly romanticized vision of knights and their lives, bring a source of hilarity but also allows us to question ourselves how do react before conflicting points of view present in our communication with others. Maybe all of us are a bit like Don Quixote or a bit like those who deem him wrong, not being able to put ourselves in some other person's place. </span>
  <a data-text-id="9147711290279776284" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2846125272" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer2754209942047838784"><br/><br/><br/><pre> Δείτε την κριτική στα ελληνικά στις <a href='https://kyriakosorokkou.wordpress.com/2020/06/28/%ce%b4%ce%bf%ce%bd-%ce%ba%ce%b9%cf%87%cf%8e%cf%84%ce%b7%cf%82/' rel='nofollow noopener'>βιβλιοαλχημείες</a><br/><br/>While having a break at the cafeteria a nice morning in May, I was surfing on my phone and discovered Public (book)Stores in Cyprus were celebrating their 5 years of online presence, with really low prices. This 1st volume was just €10 from the €26 it is originally, so I bought it with no further ado.<br/><br/>I bought the 2nd volume on September 29, my name day and (possibly) Miguel de Cervantes' birthday.<br/><br/>The low price was the first reason I bought this</pre></span>
  <span id="freeText2754209942047838784" style="display:none"><br /><br /><br /><pre> Δείτε την κριτική στα ελληνικά στις <a href="https://kyriakosorokkou.wordpress.com/2020/06/28/%ce%b4%ce%bf%ce%bd-%ce%ba%ce%b9%cf%87%cf%8e%cf%84%ce%b7%cf%82/" rel="nofollow noopener">βιβλιοαλχημείες</a><br /><br />While having a break at the cafeteria a nice morning in May, I was surfing on my phone and discovered Public (book)Stores in Cyprus were celebrating their 5 years of online presence, with really low prices. This 1st volume was just €10 from the €26 it is originally, so I bought it with no further ado.<br /><br />I bought the 2nd volume on September 29, my name day and (possibly) Miguel de Cervantes' birthday.<br /><br />The low price was the first reason I bought this edition.<br />The second reason was that this volume and the next are illustrated by my favourite surrealist painter Salvador Dali, also from Spain.<br />With 31 coloured and 37 black-and-white illustrations if I counted correctly.<br /><br />This was the last book I read last September and the first in October.<br />In other words I read the 1st volume the last week of September and the 2nd volume in the first week of October. I read 1705! pages in 15 days which is a really good reading pace.<br /><br />I usually prefer to read a book in one paperback volume instead of being divided in one or two, and in hardback. but with Don Quixote I made an exception because of price, Dali, and because they were originally published separately by a ten year difference.<br /><br />If Don Quixote wasn't as successful as it was when it was published in 1605, with 6 more reprints in the same year, and translations in English (1612) and French (1614) Cervantes wouldn't have written the next part. He felt that the 1st part was complete and a stand alone. <br />Both parts are now published in one volume regardless of their 10-year gap<br /><br />Thus, because of the 1st part's success he decided to write and publish the 2nd part, in 1615 a year before his death, the same date with the great English playwright William Shakespeare, 23 April 1616, a date celebrated as World Book Day.<br /><br />The second part of my review continues on the second part of Don Quixote, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2995850473" rel="nofollow noopener">here</a>.</pre></span>

<div id="review_1725126493" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1725126493" class="readable"
              >

<div id="review_1468934155" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1468934155" />
    <a title="Ignacio" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2670004-ignacio"><img alt="Ignacio" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1395010521p2/2670004.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1468934155?book_show_action=true">Dec 14, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ignacio" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ignacio" href="/user/show/2670004-ignacio">Ignacio</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9788437622149" href="/book/show/69618.Don_Quijote_de_la_Mancha_I">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1468934155" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5005176091450921787">A lot of readers seem to lose interest by the end of the First Part of Don Quixote and I’m not sure how many make it through the Second Part. It can be very challenging to try to read the whole book through. Personally, I prefer to treat them as separate novels, not so much as parts (like the books of, say, the Lord of the Rings) but more like the relation between The Hobbit and LOTR. I just finished rereading the First Part and plan to wait at least six months or a year before I reread the Seco</span>
  <span id="freeText5005176091450921787" style="display:none">A lot of readers seem to lose interest by the end of the First Part of Don Quixote and I’m not sure how many make it through the Second Part. It can be very challenging to try to read the whole book through. Personally, I prefer to treat them as separate novels, not so much as parts (like the books of, say, the Lord of the Rings) but more like the relation between The Hobbit and LOTR. I just finished rereading the First Part and plan to wait at least six months or a year before I reread the Second Part. <br /><br />The first half of the book is one tragicomic misadventure after another and moves at a very fast pace. It is only once the “road novel” aspect stops and characters begin to converge at the inn and tell (or read) stories that the pace really slows down and we plunge into the romantic tales of Cardenio, Dorotea, Luscinda, the captive, and others. <br /><br />I read it in Spanish, so the physical and verbal humor are hilarious to me. But perhaps you have to be a native speaker or bilingual to fully appreciate the flavor of Cervantes’ verbal wit (I would call it “gracia” in Spanish, which is grace in the sense of wit, charm, flair). There are several reviews where people are complaining about the excess of “slapstick” in the book, as if this did not belong in a classic work of literature. They forget that slapstick can be sublime, as in Chaplin or the Marx Brothers. The physical humor between don Quixote and Sancho is like a breath of fresh air – enriched by the verbal humor and the endless parody of discursive styles. Like with James Joyce, it is hard to tell where the irony begins and ends, since between the jokes Cervantes gives us much of his compassionate humanism. <br /><br />Rereading it this time, I noticed that the priest has a very important role – he might even be seen as the real protagonist of the novel: he often engineers adventures and practical jokes, which he enjoys immensely, just as he does reading and critiquing literature! He seems to be in the background orchestrating a lot of what is going on, and thus creating and enjoying much of the adventure. <br /><br />It is true the novel gets heavy once it introduces romantic couple after romantic couple, including some pastoral-style narratives. But the story of the captive (based on Cervantes’ own captivity in Algiers) was so new at the time and must have been tremendously fascinating to his audience. In his introduction to the novel (in the Cátedra Spanish edition), John Jay Allen explains how these slower episodes allowed Cervantes to develop a modern aspect of the novel: his interest in introspection and psychological exploration – think of the psychology of jealousy, for example. <br /><br />In addition to these romantic novellas embedded within Don Quixote, there are also episodes that are purely discursive: literary critique (not only of romances of chivalry), speeches, such as the Discourse on Arms and Letters, etc. It is important to remember that this is one of the first, if not the first, modern novel: the form of the novel was not settled yet. It was a flexible literary form that incorporated pastoral novellas, romances, the genre of “exemplary novels” dear to Cervantes, discourses, critiques, etc. It is only in the Second Part of Don Quixote that Cervantes composes a more unified form that is easier to recognize as a novel by our modern standards. </span>
  <a data-text-id="5005176091450921787" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1794916957" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1794916957" />
    <a title="Brian Eshleman" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5862823-brian-eshleman"><img alt="Brian Eshleman" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1591296763p2/5862823.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1794916957?book_show_action=true">Oct 26, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brian Eshleman" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brian Eshleman" href="/user/show/5862823-brian-eshleman">Brian Eshleman</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover  " href="/book/show/5977837-don-quixote-of-the-mancha-part-i">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1794916957" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11705032643840549442">I probably don't bring a five-star palette to this work that so many others have found unsurpassed, but I enjoyed it nevertheless. I counsel college students, and I also recently read Stroke of Insight on the effectiveness of an empathetic approach to treating mental impairment, so readers of my reviews will forgive me if I take the hammer of my perspective and find nails even in a timeless work that has much to say beyond my frame of reference.<br/><br/>I find the gracious way in which the author and the</span>
  <span id="freeText11705032643840549442" style="display:none">I probably don't bring a five-star palette to this work that so many others have found unsurpassed, but I enjoyed it nevertheless. I counsel college students, and I also recently read Stroke of Insight on the effectiveness of an empathetic approach to treating mental impairment, so readers of my reviews will forgive me if I take the hammer of my perspective and find nails even in a timeless work that has much to say beyond my frame of reference.<br /><br />I find the gracious way in which the author and the squire that is the product of his pen dealt with Don Quixote's mental illness to be endearing. Sancho Panza seems to find a way to keep intact the Don Quixote's sense of the color and flavor of the world WITHOUT allowing him to get himself into much dreadful danger. What a guide for the counselor, the parent, the coach or anyone hoping to inspire, protect, and support maturation! Cervantes is more fun to read than William Glasser, but he predates him with the same idea of figuring out the picture inside the head of the person we want to influence and helping them achieve that – even by a different route.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11705032643840549442" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1741247882" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1741247882" />
    <a title="j" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17503323-j"><img alt="j" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1600691471p2/17503323.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1741247882?book_show_action=true">Oct 12, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="j" class="user" itemprop="url" name="j" href="/user/show/17503323-j">j</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9788437622149" href="/book/show/69618.Don_Quijote_de_la_Mancha_I">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17503323-j?shelf=surprisingly-i-liked-it">surprisingly-i-liked-it</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17503323-j?shelf=assigned-reading">assigned-reading</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1741247882" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11616281952421064509">3.8 stars<br/><br/>i had to read this for class and the teacher divided it into two parts so i will first talk about it in those individual parts before talking about both. (i read it in spanish so the names and places and other stuff will be in spanish)<br/><br/>first part (chap.1-chap.22)<br/>writing:<br/>i can't really complain much here. the writing was really good, sometimes the jokes fell flat, but when i laughed, i laughed. the only problems i had per se were with the very old words that i didn't know the meaning to. </span>
  <span id="freeText11616281952421064509" style="display:none">3.8 stars<br /><br />i had to read this for class and the teacher divided it into two parts so i will first talk about it in those individual parts before talking about both. (i read it in spanish so the names and places and other stuff will be in spanish)<br /><br />first part (chap.1-chap.22)<br />writing:<br />i can't really complain much here. the writing was really good, sometimes the jokes fell flat, but when i laughed, i laughed. the only problems i had per se were with the very old words that i didn't know the meaning to. and also this is more of a me thing, but i have a horrible time concentrating and when a character (don quijote) started monologuing about whatever, by the time we got to the middle of the monologue, i would have already forgotten what he was talking about, so i had to go back and reread those parts.<br />like i said more of a me thing.<br /><br />characters:<br />i love don quijote!!!!!!!and sancho!!!!!! i didn't really dislike any characters, but i loved these two and their dynamic was so much. the moments i did laugh were mostly due to the convos between them. another character that made me laugh so much was ginés de pasamonte. that whole chapter killed me. it was golden. marcela was another great character. when her very feminist (might i say) speech started, i got so excited. she was very well done and i thought it was a very intelligent thing to first show how these (pissbabies) men saw her before having her come in, say her piece, and then give no fucks and leave without listening to anyone. i love u marcela<br /><br />story: <br />everyone knows what don quijote is about. this one fifty year old guy reads so many books about knights that he goes insane and goes around thinking he's a knight. before starting this, i thought i was gonna be really bored, but i actually really liked it. there were some adventures i enjoyed more than the others (for example, the prisoner ones, the ones with the sheep, los molinos,etc) and others i found kind of boring, but in general i enjoyed this part sufficiently.<br /><br />rating: 4<br /><br />part 2 (chap.23-chap.52)<br /><br />writing:<br />again, it was really good. it was way funnier than the first half. by the time i got to this part, i was already used to the language and style so it wasn't as confusing as the first part had been. there were still some monologues here. i enjoyed them more here than in the first part.<br /><br />characters:<br />here's where some of my beef with this book begins.<br /><br />i love don quijote's character and sancho panza's so when they were like whisked away and we were hearing about this character i did not know, nor cared about, it was hard for me to enjoy the story as much. i didn't give a shit about cardenio, dorotea, luscinda, don fernando and so on. the only characters i wanted to hear about were the ones we met in the first part not these randos. <br /><br />however, i loved don quijote and sancho even more in this part and whenever we saw characters from the first part, i got very excited.<br /><br />story:<br />now, like i said, i didn't care about most of the characters, so i got so bored with their stories. i actually skipped a few of pages explaining the love thing going on between cardenio, luscinda, dorotea, and don fernando. it was just too much for me. but when we got the characters we already knew, the story was really good. i really liked it when don quijote wrote the letter to dulcinea. and even though, i didn't really care about the new characters, i liked their plot to get don quijote back home. i found it really funny, especially when they put him in the cage.<br /><br />rating: 3.75<br /><br />other thoughts:<br />-this took me three months to read because the exams were a month apart and damn did that affect my enjoyment of the story. at some parts it felt like a chore having to read this. <br />-i barely passed the first exam, fuck me uo quijote<br /><br />tl;dr: i really liked the story and found it quite fun, even though there were parts that were boring to me. most of the characters were fun, others were...,,,...,.not. overall, i'm really glad i read this.</span>

<div id="review_1550440644" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1550440644" />
    <a title="Jeremy Ngadiman3" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/51339086-jeremy-ngadiman3"><img alt="Jeremy Ngadiman3" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1550440644?book_show_action=true">Feb 16, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jeremy Ngadiman3" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jeremy Ngadiman3" href="/user/show/51339086-jeremy-ngadiman3">Jeremy Ngadiman3</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781492271604" href="/book/show/21968358-don-quixote-volume-1">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/51339086-jeremy-ngadiman3?shelf=classic">classic</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1550440644" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13466360849000295278">Don Quixote: the story of an average nobleman who believes, outside of sanity, that he is to become a knight at a time where Spain, all of Europe probably, no longer had knights, and his books on chivalry and legendary knights were to blame. Despite the efforts of the cleric, barber, housemaid, and his niece, Alonso Quijano (or Quesada, or Quijada) changed his name to Don Quixote, took on his grandfather's armor, mounted his not-so-impressive steed Rocinante, got himself knighted in an inn which</span>
  <span id="freeText13466360849000295278" style="display:none">Don Quixote: the story of an average nobleman who believes, outside of sanity, that he is to become a knight at a time where Spain, all of Europe probably, no longer had knights, and his books on chivalry and legendary knights were to blame. Despite the efforts of the cleric, barber, housemaid, and his niece, Alonso Quijano (or Quesada, or Quijada) changed his name to Don Quixote, took on his grandfather's armor, mounted his not-so-impressive steed Rocinante, got himself knighted in an inn which he took for a castle, and took Sancho Panza, the local simpleton, as his squire to accompany him on his great quest for the revival of chivalry, the enforcement of justice, and the love of Dulcinea del Toboso, a village farm girl who neither knows him nor is aware of his self-imposed quest. And yes, this book is a classic.<br /><br />The humor and craziness behind Don Quixote was the product of Miguel de Cervantes Saavedra, a soldier, slave, poet, and nobleman whose life story (if you actually read the translator's preface) greatly affected the elements of the book. One of the things he would do was make characters offhandedly mention or praise him for something he actually did in real life such as write a certain poem or escape slavery in Tunisia. Another thing he would do is display great amounts of sarcasm. This was displayed by the fact that Don Quixote's ridiculous story was narrated from the point of view of a historian who was genuinely in awe of him, and there were numerous poets with notable titles who praised him and his endeavors. Lastly, Cervantes would actually make Don Quixote have logical arguments with other characters concerning the value of chivalry and knighthood which seems like Cervantes had just inserted an essay arguing for his beliefs into his novel, and the notion made the story all the more entertaining.<br /><br />I find the book amusing, if not hilarious. The sarcasm behind the story's narration was very entertaining, and the references on Cervantes' life through the plot or a character's comment was like finding a needle in a haystack, the needle being your favorite needle. Meanwhile, the characters were very diverse and range from various backgrounds and spheres of life, and I really enjoyed how Sancho would often state the obvious or give simple - but witty - comments to Don Quixote's deluded words and actions, often at the expense of the knight himself. <br /><br />Sadly, I didn't like everything in the book. It always saddens me to see Don Quixote suffering from his delusions, and I especially pitied the fact that he was quite well-learned and knowledgeable when discussing things outside of chivalry. Then, the diversity of the book included several awkward parts when discussing the romance or situations concerning the lives of the minor characters, but it is sometimes used to ridicule the love of the knights in the legends. Lastly, Cervantes makes a lot of Spanish, mythological, Latin, or historical references which I could not always grasp on several occasions, but that is because Don Quixote was published in the 15th century.....and I am too ignorant to understand them all.<br /><br />At the end of the day, I would say that Don Quixote was more than a humorous comedy from the past. Cervantes wrote the book as an argument against the idea that chivalry should not be too esteemed by society and intellectuals, but a deeper theme in the book was the idea that literature is not always a great reflection of reality. As we delve into fantasy, sometimes it is important to take a pause and remind ourselves that we live in reality, and reality may have surprises of its own just waiting to be found.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13466360849000295278" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1470162320" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1470162320" />
    <a title="Adam Smith" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3514295-adam-smith"><img alt="Adam Smith" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442981075p2/3514295.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1470162320?book_show_action=true">Dec 16, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Adam Smith" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Adam Smith" href="/user/show/3514295-adam-smith">Adam Smith</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781400093007" href="/book/show/57410.Don_Quijote_de_la_Mancha">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3514295-adam-smith?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3514295-adam-smith?shelf=reading-challenge-2016">reading-challenge-2016</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1470162320" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1880337834166772661">A nobleman of a village in La Mancha reads one too many books on chivalry and convinces himself that his purpose in life is to become a knight errant and ride the countryside in search of adventure. Together with his trusty squire Sancho Panza, and being pursued by a barber and a curate from his home village, Don Quixote sallies forth on a quest to bring the light of chivalry to a world much in need.<br/><br/>I hated this book. I'd heard such good things about it, but aside from a small number of iconic s</span>
  <span id="freeText1880337834166772661" style="display:none">A nobleman of a village in La Mancha reads one too many books on chivalry and convinces himself that his purpose in life is to become a knight errant and ride the countryside in search of adventure. Together with his trusty squire Sancho Panza, and being pursued by a barber and a curate from his home village, Don Quixote sallies forth on a quest to bring the light of chivalry to a world much in need.<br /><br />I hated this book. I'd heard such good things about it, but aside from a small number of iconic scenes, everything in this book just grated on my nerves. Maybe I'm missing something, but the overarching theme of <b>"All fiction is evil!"</b> was something I could not swallow. Willful ignorance is, in my opinion, an intolerable sin, but that seems to be the message this book is preaching. The ravenous fanaticism during the book burning scene in the early chapters was painfully enough that I almost stopped reading.<br /><br />The bulk of the story is made up of Don Quixote getting the crap kicked out of him followed by several pages of unnecessary sonnets. All I can take away from this is that people back then must have been insufferably bored every hour of the day. Not a scene goes by where they aren't reciting some poem or sonnet or song or somebody's entire life story plus that of their several friends. Wherever they go they seem to feel the need to stop and tell each other every single detail about anyone they ever met or glanced at in passing.<br /><br />I suppose this is supposed to be a satire, but without the context that must have been apparent when this was written, it just felt lowbrow and mean spirited. And that's not even touching upon the values dissonance brought about by its age. All the female characters are either fainting ninnies or spiteful temptresses that should be despised for not marrying the first man to ask. I get that marital values had very different means back then, but Don Fernando's story in particular struck me as wrong, he essentially coerced a girl into marrying him so he could have his way with her, ditched her, then set out to steal his best friend's girl, and I'm supposed to be happy when the first girl convinces him to take her back? Also going to ignore Sancho's decision that he is going to sell any black servants he might end up with into slavery so he can be governor over a more respectable colour.<br /><br />The book was probably pretty radical when it came out, and it is pretty much the ur example for people delving too deep into fiction and trying to make it real, but I just couldn't bring myself to like it. It just dragged on and on and on and on. I doubt I will be reading the second part anytime soon.<br /><br />A disappointing book that has not aged well.<br /><br />***Reading Challenge 2016: A book set more than 100 years in the past***</span>
  <a data-text-id="1880337834166772661" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1936859426" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1936859426" />
    <a title="John Frankham" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/15748696-john-frankham"><img alt="John Frankham" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1356391325p2/15748696.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1936859426?book_show_action=true">Mar 13, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="John Frankham" class="user" itemprop="url" name="John Frankham" href="/user/show/15748696-john-frankham">John Frankham</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15748696-john-frankham?shelf=fiction-pre-1900">fiction-pre-1900</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1936859426" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13315271989594125226"> This is the original book from 1605. 450 pages. The GR blurb says:<br/><br/>"Don Quixote has become so entranced by reading chivalric romances, that he determines to become a knight-errant himself. In the company of his faithful squire, Sancho Panza, his exploits blossom in all sorts of wonderful ways. While Quixote's fancy often leads him astray – he tilts at windmills, imagining them to be giants – Sancho acquires cunning and a certain sagacity. Sane madman and wise fool, they roam the world together, </span>
  <span id="freeText13315271989594125226" style="display:none"> This is the original book from 1605. 450 pages. The GR blurb says:<br /><br />"Don Quixote has become so entranced by reading chivalric romances, that he determines to become a knight-errant himself. In the company of his faithful squire, Sancho Panza, his exploits blossom in all sorts of wonderful ways. While Quixote's fancy often leads him astray – he tilts at windmills, imagining them to be giants – Sancho acquires cunning and a certain sagacity. Sane madman and wise fool, they roam the world together, and together they have haunted readers' imaginations for nearly four hundred years. 1With its experimental form and literary playfulness, Don Quixote generally has been recognized as the first modern novel."<br /><br />For me, this was a real slog. While recognising its originality, it has to be said that it is a one trick pony. Repetitious and overlong, the humour is slapstick and hit or miss. We, clever readers, are complicit in the sympathetic and knowing mockery of our hero.<br /><br />If Cervantes is as great as Shakespeare, as often said, then Shakespeare has a very little to say about the human condition. <br /><br />Will I read the second volume? Not until I have a lot of time on my hands!</span>
  <a data-text-id="13315271989594125226" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2077696491" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2077696491" />
    <a title="Robert Cohen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/20667282-robert-cohen"><img alt="Robert Cohen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1392931054p2/20667282.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2077696491?book_show_action=true">Jul 31, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Robert Cohen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Robert Cohen" href="/user/show/20667282-robert-cohen">Robert Cohen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781406791723" href="/book/show/1382972.Don_Quixote_de_La_Mancha_Vol_1">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2077696491" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5439914813550571514">The complete Don Quixote is a monumental undertaking, so I have decided to present this review based only on Part 1, which I have just completed. I will take a break and will follow with a review of Part II at a later time.<br/><br/>No less a scholar than Harold Bloom calls Don Quixote, “still the best of all novels”, and also comments, “this great book contains within itself all the novels that have followed in its sublime wake”. Edith Grossman, whose translation is recommended by Bloom and is the one I </span>
  <span id="freeText5439914813550571514" style="display:none">The complete Don Quixote is a monumental undertaking, so I have decided to present this review based only on Part 1, which I have just completed. I will take a break and will follow with a review of Part II at a later time.<br /><br />No less a scholar than Harold Bloom calls Don Quixote, “still the best of all novels”, and also comments, “this great book contains within itself all the novels that have followed in its sublime wake”. Edith Grossman, whose translation is recommended by Bloom and is the one I read, calls Don Quixote, “the first – and probably the greatest – modern novel”. I concur with these statements.<br /><br />A word on translations. This is no small issue. It is rather the ONLY issue when reading a great book such as Don Quixote or Anna Karenina. There have been 16 or 17 translations of Don Quixote since the publication of Part 1 in 1605 and Part 2 in 1615. I confess to generally being drawn to whatever is free on Amazon, and in this case it would be the John Ormsby translation from 1885. I don’t have the patience to read an entire translation, and then read another one to compare, especially in as epic a book as Don Quixote. So I sampled. I found the Ormsby to be plodding and literal, whereas the Grossman is breezy, modern, and delightfully funny. I actually spent the $10 for the Kindle Grossman translation, and it may be the best $10 I’ve ever spent. <br /><br />Translations need to be compatible with the needs of the reader. My favorite translation may not be the same as yours. Do a Google search of translations of Don Quixote, and you will find dozens of lively conversations on this subject, from Don Quixote aficionados who may have read as many as 6 or 7 different translations, and take great delight in comparing them. These folks are erudite and can comment authoritatively. Evaluating translations is a sub-genre of the pastime of reading. Before you embark on the daunting task of reading Don Quixote, make sure you are reading a translation that keeps your attention, and excites you (plan to spend a couple of hours doing the research). If you don’t want to do the research, simply get the Grossman translation. It is wonderful. And do NOT use the old translations that are available free on Project Gutenberg. They are dreadfully dull.<br /><br />Although Don Quixote is a novel because of the overarching story of the main protagonist, it is really a collection of exceptionally well crafted short stories, each of which could easily stand on its own. I found that I could read several chapters, take a break to read something else, and then come back, without missing a beat. This may or may not work for you, but it worked for me. When I thought I had encountered the ne-plus-ultra of humorous and/or poignant stories, the very next story disabused me of that notion. This is truly the greatest modern novel.<br /><br />I was particularly moved by the tragedy of Anselmo, Lotario, and Camila. Somewhat melodramatic (as are many of the stories), this is a cautionary tale whose moral is “Oh what a tangled web we weave………………….”. This story is a mini Shakespearean tragedy (ironically, Cervantes and Shakespeare died on the very same day, April 23, 1616).<br /><br />There is no reason to be intimidated by this book. Cervantes didn’t write it to torture readers 400 years later. Read about his life to get a sense of why the book is the masterpiece it is. Yes, it is very long, but it’s doubtful it will be the longest book you will ever read, and certainly not the longest book out there (Proust’s 4215 page “In Search of Lost Time”, anyone?), and its entertainment quotient far exceeds that of other long novels such as Moby Dick or Bleak House (apologies to Dickens fans, of which I am not one).<br /><br />When you have finished Don Quixote Part 1, keep in mind Bloom’s comment, quoted earlier: “this great book contains within itself all the novels that have followed in its sublime wake”. It will be difficult for you to read the later classics without a spark or two igniting in your head, reminding you of the pace, architecture, humor, eloquence, transcendent dialogue, and sheer joy of Don Quixote.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5439914813550571514" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText14257520777383139337" style="display:none">Book one done! <br /><br />That was such an undertaking that I don't know how to summarize the experience. <span id="freeTextContainer1990516383249606333">I just finished "Don Quixote, Volume 1" by Miguel de Cervantes Saavedra. Before reading this well-known classic, I had absolutely no preconceived notion about the story. I was delighted to find it much more a comedy than an adventure. Quixote is a hotheaded buffoon, and Sancho Panza his unwitting and naive accomplice. I just love seeing idiocy interacting with seriousness. I think the main point that Cervantes conveys is the dangerous effect that literature (or media in general) has upon people </span>
  <span id="freeText1990516383249606333" style="display:none">I just finished "Don Quixote, Volume 1" by Miguel de Cervantes Saavedra. Before reading this well-known classic, I had absolutely no preconceived notion about the story. I was delighted to find it much more a comedy than an adventure. Quixote is a hotheaded buffoon, and Sancho Panza his unwitting and naive accomplice. I just love seeing idiocy interacting with seriousness. I think the main point that Cervantes conveys is the dangerous effect that literature (or media in general) has upon people who have a mental illness, as I believe Quixote had. Here's a quote: "I do not deny that what happened to us is a thing worth laughing at. But it is not worth telling, for not everyone is sufficiently intelligent to be able to see things from the right point of view."</span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
